Key areas for improvement: 1. **Better tile sizes**: The current configs might not be optimal 2. **Kernel launch overhead**: Each expert's tile map computation involves a Python loop 3. **Grid size**: Too many or too few blocks 4. **Memory access patterns**: Might be suboptimal 5. **Occupancy**: Shared memory usage might limit occupancy Let me think about what optimizations would give the biggest gains: 1. **Larger tiles**: Using BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=256 with more warps could improve throughput 2. **Better BLOCK_K**: Using BLOCK_K=64 or 128 to reduce K-loop iterations 3. **Occupancy tuning**: Ensure we're not limited by registers or shared memory Actually, 33.7 TFLOPS represents only about 7% of peak. That's very low. Let me check what could be bottlenecking: 1. Is the kernel compute-bound or memory-bound? - For shape 0: FLOPs = 2 * T_total * H * (2*I) = 2 * 32768 * 4096 * 3072 = 824B FLOPs - Bytes = T_total*K*H*2 + E*H*(2*I)*2 + T_total*K*I*2 = 262144*4096*2 + 128*4096*3072*2 + 262144*1536*2 = 2.15GB + 3.22GB + 0.81GB ≈ 6.18GB - Arithmetic intensity = 824B / 6.18GB ≈ 133 FLOPs/byte - At 1.8 TB/s bandwidth, time = 6.18GB / 1.8TB/s = 3.4 ms (bandwidth limited) - At 500 TFLOPS, time = 824B / 500T = 1.6 ms (compute limited) Wait, this doesn't add up. Let me recalculate. Actually, the FLOPs formula from problem.yaml is: `2 * T_total * H * (2 * I)`. For shape 0: 2 * 32768 * 4096 * (2 * 1536) = 2 * 32768 * 4096 * 3072 = 824,633,720,832 ≈ 825 billion FLOPs Time = 24.4 ms, so achieved TFLOPS = 825B / 24.4ms / 1e12 = 33.8 TFLOPS ✓ Bandwidth: bytes_formula = T_total*K*H*2 + E*H*(2*I)*2 + T_total*K*I*2 Actually, the problem counts each token visiting K experts separately. T_perm = T_total * K = 32768 * 8 = 262144 Bytes = 262144*4096*2 + 128*4096*(2*1536)*2 + 262144*1536*2 = 2,147,483,648 + 128*4096*3072*2 + 805,306,368 = 2.15GB + 3.22GB + 0.81GB ≈ 6.18 GB At 1.8 TB/s: 6.18GB / 1800GB/s = 3.4 ms (roofline bandwidth bound) But actual time is 24.4 ms, which is 7x slower than the bandwidth roofline. This suggests the kernel is neither compute-efficient nor memory-efficient. The issue is probably: - Low occupancy (not enough warps to hide latency) - Poor memory coalescing - The double-load of weights (both W_gate and W_up for each K tile) - Grid inefficiency Let me think about what specific optimizations would help: 1. **The tile map pre-computation has a Python loop over E (128 iterations)**. The `_build_tile_map` function iterates over experts in Python. This is fast (microseconds) but let me double-check. 2. **The kernel loads both W_gate and W_up tiles in the inner loop**. Since both use the same x tile, this is correct. But loading two weight tiles per K iteration doubles the memory traffic. 3. **Grid dimensions**: For shape 0 with BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128: - num_m_tiles = sum(ceil(n_e / 128)) ≈ 128 * ceil(2048/128) = 128 * 16 = 2048 - num_n_tiles = ceil(1536/128) = 12 - Total blocks = 2048 * 12 = 24576 - With 188 SMs: 131 blocks per SM - Each block: 8 warps * 32 threads = 256 threads - Per SM: 131 * 256 / 1536 max threads... wait, SMs can't run 131 blocks concurrently. They can run maybe 4-6 blocks concurrently depending on resources. - Actually, the GPU runs blocks in waves. With 188 SMs, the first wave runs 188*6 ≈ 1128 blocks, then the next wave, etc. - 24576 / 1128 ≈ 22 waves. Each wave takes ~1ms. Hmm, but 22 waves * some time per wave = 24ms. That could explain the performance! 4. **Key issue: shared memory pressure**. For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128, BLOCK_K=32 with num_warps=8: - Each block needs shared memory for the MMA tiles - With double accumulators (gate + up), register pressure doubles - Triton might be spilling to local memory Let me think about how to improve: **Option A**: Use smaller tiles to increase occupancy - BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64, BLOCK_K=64, num_warps=4 - More blocks but each uses fewer resources - Better occupancy **Option B**: Use larger K tiles to reduce memory traffic - BLOCK_K=128 means loading x once for 128 K elements instead of 64 - Reduces the number of K-loop iterations - But increases shared memory usage **Option C**: Sequential K iteration within larger outer tile - Process more work per block to reduce scheduling overhead **Option D**: Fuse the weight loading - pack W_gate and W_up - Instead of loading W_gate and W_up separately, pack them as (H, 2*I) and load once - Then split for the two MMA operations - This reduces memory traffic by 50% for weights Wait, Option D is a great idea! Instead of loading two (BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N) tiles (one for gate, one for up), load one (BLOCK_K, 2*BLOCK_N) tile that contains both. Then do: - gate = dot(x, w_combined[:, :N]) - up = dot(x, w_combined[:, N:]) But Triton's tl.dot doesn't easily support slicing the B matrix this way. However, I could load the combined weight and then split it in software before passing to tl.dot. Actually, I can't easily slice within tl.dot. But I could load a wider weight tile and call tl.dot twice with different views. The x tile is the same. Hmm, but the memory access pattern for loading the combined weight would be better (one load instead of two). Let me think about whether Triton supports this... Actually, for the grouped GEMM kernel, the weights are (E, H, I) for each of gate and up. I can't easily combine them without changing the weight layout. But I CAN combine the loads: Currently: ```python w_gate = tl.load(W_gate_ptr + wg_off, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0) w_up = tl.load(W_up_ptr + wu_off, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0) ``` These are two separate loads from different memory locations. If I interleave gate and up weights in memory, I could load them as one contiguous chunk. But the weight layout is fixed by the Model interface. Alternative: what if I load twice as many columns? Instead of BLOCK_N columns of gate and BLOCK_N columns of up separately, I could handle gate and up computation in the same kernel by computing half the columns for gate and half for up, then doing another pass for the remaining columns. That doesn't really help. Let me think about other optimizations: **Optimization 1: Use `tl.dot` with wider accumulators** Actually, I already have two separate accumulators. The issue is the dual weight load. **Optimization 2: Reduce grid scheduling overhead** With 24576 blocks and 188 SMs, each block runs quickly but scheduling overhead is high. If I process more N tiles per block (loop over N internally), I can reduce the number of blocks. For example, with BLOCK_M=64, I could internally loop over N tiles: ```python for n_tile in range(num_n_tiles): rn = n_tile * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) # compute and store ``` This reduces the total blocks from num_m_tiles * num_n_tiles to just num_m_tiles. With 2048 M tiles, that's 2048 blocks across 188 SMs = 11 per SM. Much better scheduling! But wait, this means each block does more work (all N tiles), so each block runs longer. The total work is the same but scheduling overhead is reduced. This should help. **Optimization 3: Better BLOCK_K to reduce K iterations** With BLOCK_K=128 and H=4096: 32 K iterations (instead of 64 with BLOCK_K=64) Let me try combining: internal N loop + larger BLOCK_K. Actually, let me also think about the register pressure. With two accumulators (gate and up), we use 2 * BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N * 4 bytes / (num_warps * 32 threads) registers per thread. For BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=128, num_warps=8: - Each accumulator: 64*128 = 8192 elements - 8192 * 4 bytes / (8*32) = 128 elements per thread = 128 registers per accumulator - Two accumulators = 256 registers per thread - SM120 has 65536 registers per SM, so 65536/256 = 256 threads = 8 warps per SM - That's just 1 block per SM (since each block has 8 warps = 256 threads) - SM has 1536 max threads, so occupancy would be 256/1536 = 17% That's terrible occupancy! We need to reduce register usage. For BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=64, num_warps=4: - Each accumulator: 64*64 = 4096 elements - 4096 * 4 / (4*32) = 128 elements per thread = 128 registers per accumulator - Two accumulators = 256 registers per thread - With 4 warps = 128 threads per block - 65536 / 256 = 256 threads per SM = 2 blocks per SM - Occupancy: 256/1536 = 17% Same problem! The double accumulator is killing occupancy. Hmm, but Triton might be smart about this. The actual register usage depends on how Triton schedules the MMA operations. Maybe the two accumulators can reuse registers between the inner and outer loops. Actually, wait. In our kernel, `final_gate` and `final_up` are persistent accumulators. The MMA accumulators `mma_gate` and `mma_up` are recreated each outer loop iteration. So at any point, we have: - 2 "final" accumulators (persistent) - 2 "mma" accumulators (within the current inner loop) That's 4 accumulators in the inner loop! That's a LOT of registers. But wait, can Triton reuse registers between mma_gate and mma_up? In the inner loop: ```python mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate) mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up) ``` These are sequential operations. After `mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate)`, the MMA hardware accumulates into mma_gate registers. Then `mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up)` accumulates into mma_up registers. The x and w loads happen before both. Triton should be able to schedule the MMA operations so that mma_gate and mma_up don't need to be live simultaneously in the inner loop. But in practice, the Triton compiler might not be that smart. Actually, there's another issue. The tl.dot instruction on SM120 with mma.sync.aligned requires the accumulator to be in a specific set of registers. Two separate accumulators means two sets of MMA accumulator registers, which means the compiler needs to either: 1. Keep both alive (double register pressure) 2. Spill one to shared memory 3. Use different register sets for different iterations For SM120, each warp has 4 sets of accumulator registers (or maybe more on Blackwell). So the compiler might be able to use different sets for gate and up. In any case, the high register pressure is likely limiting occupancy and performance. **What can I do about it?** Option 1: Combine gate and up into a single accumulator by doubling BLOCK_N - Load W_gate and W_up as adjacent in memory? They're not adjacent. - But I could manually interleave the computation: - For each K tile, compute gate contribution to first half of BLOCK_N, then up contribution to second half - Requires loading different weight tiles Actually, this is equivalent to what we already have, just organized differently. Option 2: Fuse gate and up computation into alternating iterations - Odd K iterations: accumulate into gate accumulator - Even K iterations: accumulate into up accumulator - Uses only 1 MMA accumulator at a time - But then we need to reload x for each weight Wait, this doesn't help because we'd need both gate and up final accumulators anyway. Option 3: Sequential expert processing - Instead of computing gate(x@W_gate) and up(x@W_up) simultaneously, compute them sequentially - First pass: gate = x @ W_gate - Second pass: up = x @ W_up - Combine: silu(gate) * up - This halves the register pressure (only 1 accumulator at a time) - But doubles the number of K iterations (we reload x) - Also doubles the grid Actually, Option 3 seems very promising. Let me think about it: - Each block computes either gate OR up, not both - We alternate blocks: even blocks compute gate, odd blocks compute up - After both passes, we fuse with SwiGLU Wait, but the output needs to be SwiGLU fused. If we split gate and up computation, we need to write intermediate results somewhere and then fuse. Actually, a cleaner approach: 1. Compute gate = x @ W_gate for all tokens 2. Compute up = x @ W_up for all tokens 3. Fuse: silu(gate) * up Steps 1 and 2 can reuse the same kernel (just pass different weight pointers). Step 3 is a simple element-wise kernel. But this requires storing intermediate gate and up results (2 * T_perm * I bytes of memory) and an extra kernel launch. The memory overhead is ~1.6 GB for the headline shape. Hmm, that's a lot of extra memory. And we'd need to write the fusion kernel too. Let me think about this differently. Maybe the issue isn't register pressure but something else. Let me profile to understand the bottleneck. Actually, let me try the simplest optimization first: loop over N tiles internally within each block, reducing the total grid size. The key insight for MoE: within each expert, we have multiple tokens sharing the same weights. If we process all tokens of an expert together, we can: 1. Load a K tile of W_gate and W_up once 2. Compute x @ W_gate and x @ W_up for ALL M tiles using that K tile 3. This amortizes the weight load over many M tiles Current kernel: each (M_tile, N_tile) block loads weights independently for each K tile. So weights are loaded many times redundantly. If I process all N tiles internally for one M tile, the weight loading for K tiles is still redundant across M tiles (different blocks load the same weights). But if I process all M tiles for one expert in one block... the weights are loaded once and used for all M tiles. However, one block can't process 2048 rows efficiently. With fewer but larger blocks, scheduling is more efficient and weight reuse increases. Let me implement this approach. The kernel structure: ```python @triton.jit def kernel_v2( hidden_states_ptr, W_gate_ptr, W_up_ptr, out_ptr, expert_offsets_ptr, # (E+1,) int32 H, I, stride_hs_m, stride_hs_k, stride_wg_e, stride_wg_k, stride_wg_n, stride_wu_e, stride_wu_k, stride_wu_n, stride_out_m, stride_out_n, BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, RESET_EVERY: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) expert = pid // num_n_tiles n_tile = pid % num_n_tiles start = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert) end = tl.load(expert_offsets_ptr + expert + 1) n_e = end - start if n_e <= 0: return rn = n_tile * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) wg_off_e = expert * stride_wg_e wu_off_e = expert * stride_wu_e # Process M tiles for this expert sequentially for m_start in range(start, end, BLOCK_M): rows_in_tile = min(BLOCK_M, end - m_start) rm_off = m_start rm = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M) # Reset accumulators for this M tile final_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) final_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) for k_outer in range(0, H, BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY): mma_gate = tl.zeros(...) mma_up = tl.zeros(...) k_end = min(k_outer + BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY, H) for k in range(k_outer, k_end, BLOCK_K): rk = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_rem = H - k # Load x tile hs_off = (rm_off + rm[:, None]) * stride_hs_m + (k + rk[None, :]) * stride_hs_k x = tl.load(hidden_states_ptr + hs_off, mask=(rm[:,None]<rows_in_tile)&(rk[None,:]<k_rem), other=0.0) # Load weight tiles (shared across M tiles theoretically, but loaded each K iteration) wg_off = wg_off_e + (k + rk[:,None]) * stride_wg_k + rn[None,:] * stride_wg_n w_gate = tl.load(W_gate_ptr + wg_off, ...) wu_off = wu_off_e + (k + rk[:,None]) * stride_wu_k + rn[None,:] * stride_wu_n w_up = tl.load(W_up_ptr + wu_off, ...) mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate) mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up) final_gate += mma_gate final_up += mma_up # SwiGLU result = (final_gate * tl.sigmoid(final_gate) * final_up).to(tl.bfloat16) out_off = (rm_off + rm[:,None]) * stride_out_m + rn[None,:] * stride_out_n tl.store(out_ptr + out_off, result, mask=(rm[:,None]<rows_in_tile)&(rn[None,:]<I)) ``` Wait, but this doesn't actually reuse weights across M tiles. The inner K loop reloads weights for each M tile. The weight reuse happens because the same weight tile is used for all M tiles... but in this structure, the M loop is OUTSIDE the K loop! That means we reload x and weights for every M tile. Hmm, that's worse than the current approach for weight reuse. Let me think again. For weight reuse, I want the K loop to be OUTSIDE the M loop: ``` for k in K: load W_gate[k_tile] and W_up[k_tile] # once per K tile for m in M_tiles: load x[m_tile, k_tile] compute partial products ``` But then I need separate accumulators for each M tile, which is a lot of registers. Alternatively: ``` for k in K: load W_gate[k_tile] and W_up[k_tile] # once per K tile for m in M_tiles: load x[m_tile, k_tile] gate_acc[m] += dot(x, W_gate) up_acc[m] += dot(x, W_up) ``` This requires storing accumulators for ALL M tiles simultaneously, which is M_tiles * BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N * 2 accumulators * 4 bytes of registers. For 32 M tiles with BLOCK_M=64, BLOCK_N=128: 32 * 8192 * 2 * 4 = 2MB of registers. SM only has 256KB of register file. So this approach doesn't work due to register pressure. Alternative: instead of keeping all accumulators in registers, store partial results to shared memory between K iterations: ``` for k in K: load W_gate[k_tile] and W_up[k_tile] # to shared memory for m in M_tiles: load x[m_tile, k_tile] load partial_gate[m] from shared memory partial_gate[m] += dot(x, W_gate_from_smem) store partial_gate[m] to shared memory similarly for up ``` This uses shared memory for partial accumulators. """Quick profile of the kernel to identify bottlenecks."""
import torch
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import solution

# Headline shape
solution.T_total = 32768
solution.H = 4096
solution.I = 1536
solution.E = 128
solution.K = 8

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
init_args = solution.get_init_inputs()
model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in solution.get_inputs()]

# Warm up
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(3):
        model(*inputs)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Profile with torch.profiler
with torch.profiler.profile(
    activities=[torch.profiler.ProfilerActivity.CPU, torch.profiler.ProfilerActivity.CUDA],
    record_shapes=True,
    with_stack=True,
) as prof:
    with torch.no_grad():
        out = model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()

print(prof.key_averages().table(sort_by="cuda_time_total", row_limit=20))

# Also print kernel specific info
for evt in prof.key_averages():
    if 'triton' in evt.key.lower() or 'kernel' in evt.key.lower():
        print(f"  {evt.key}: cuda_time={evt.cuda_time_total/1e3:.2f}us, count={evt.count}")

# Check occupancy estimate
print(f"\nGPU: {torch.cuda.get_device_name(0)}")
print(f"SMs: {torch.cuda.get_device_properties(0).multi_processor_count}")

"""Run the kernel for ncu profiling."""
import torch
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import solution

# Headline shape
solution.T_total = 32768
solution.H = 4096
solution.I = 1536
solution.E = 128
solution.K = 8

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
init_args = solution.get_init_inputs()
model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in solution.get_inputs()]

# Warm up
with torch.no_grad():
    model(*inputs)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Run once more for profiling
with torch.no_grad():
    out = model(*inputs)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

Let me implement this approach. The kernel structure: ```python @triton.jit def kernel_sequential(x_ptr, wg_ptr, wu_ptr, out_ptr, ...): # Phase 1: compute gate final_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) for k_outer in range(0, H, BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY): mma_gate = tl.zeros(...) for k in range(k_outer, k_end, BLOCK_K): x = load(x_ptr + ...) w_gate = load(wg_ptr + ...) mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate) final_gate += mma_gate # Store gate to shared memory as bf16 gate_smem = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.bfloat16) # In shared memory # Actually, I need to allocate shared memory explicitly # Phase 2: compute up and fuse final_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32) for k_outer in range(0, H, BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY): mma_up = tl.zeros(...) for k in range(k_outer, k_end, BLOCK_K): x = load(x_ptr + ...) w_up = load(wu_ptr + ...) mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up) final_up += mma_up # Load gate from shared memory, fuse gate_bf16 = tl.load(gate_smem + ...) # but this is from shared memory gate_f32 = gate_bf16.to(tl.float32) result = (gate_f32 * tl.sigmoid(gate_f32) * final_up).to(tl.bfloat16) store(out_ptr + ..., result) ``` But wait, this loads x twice (once for gate pass, once for up pass). This is the problem. With 4 accumulators each needing 64 registers, the per-thread requirement exceeds the hardware limit. The compiler must spill registers to local memory (which is in L1/DRAM). These spills are slow. So the root cause is **register spilling**! Each thread needs too many registers, causing spills to local memory, which dramatically reduces performance. **Solution**: Reduce the number of live accumulator registers. Let me check: does Triton's compiler actually spill registers, or does it keep the accumulators in the hardware accumulator registers (which are separate from the general-purpose register file)? On NVIDIA GPUs with tensor cores, the MMA accumulator registers are separate from GPRs. For SM80+: - Each warp has 64K (65536) 32-bit GPRs per SM, shared across warps - Accumulator registers might be a different resource Actually, looking at the PTX ISA, the `.f32` accumulator registers for MMA are mapped to specific register ranges. For `mma.sync.aligned.m16n8k16`, the accumulator uses 4 consecutive f32 registers per thread. The key question: are accumulator registers allocated from the same pool as general-purpose registers? I believe they are on SM80+ - the accumulator values are stored in regular registers. In any case, the ncu output clearly shows register pressure limiting occupancy to 16.7%. Let me address this. I think the fastest path to improvement is to switch to sequential processing (compute gate, store intermediate, compute up, fuse). +@triton.jit
+def _swiglu_fuse_kernel(
+    gate_ptr,    # (T_perm, I) bf16
+    up_ptr,      # (T_perm, I) bf16
+    out_ptr,     # (T_perm, I) bf16
+    N,           # total elements = T_perm * I
+    BLOCK: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """Compute silu(gate) * up element-wise."""
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    offs = pid * BLOCK + tl.arange(0, BLOCK)
+    mask = offs < N
+

Worse! The performance dropped. The sequential approach with three kernel launches is slower because: 1. It loads x twice (once for gate, once for up) 2. It has two GEMM kernel launches + one fusion kernel launch 3. The intermediate buffers consume memory bandwidth The original dual-accumulator approach was 21ms, and this is 29ms. The extra x loads and kernel launch overhead more than offset any occupancy gains. Let me go back to the dual-accumulator approach but with better tile sizes and focus on reducing register spilling. Actually, let me think about what sized tiles would keep registers under the limit. For the dual-accumulator approach, we have 4 accumulators in the inner loop: - final_gate (persistent) - final_up (persistent) - mma_gate (temporary, recreated each outer iteration) - mma_up (temporary, recreated each outer iteration) Wait, actually, do mma_gate and mma_up need to coexist? The inner loop does: ``` mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate) mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up) ``` These are sequential. The compiler should be able to reuse registers between mma_gate and mma_up. The constraint is the two PERSISTENT accumulators (final_gate and final_up). So the effective register count per thread is: - 2 persistent accumulators × (BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N / (num_warps * 32)) registers - Plus 1 temporary accumulator (mma_gate/mma_up can share) - Plus x, w_gate, w_up loads For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=64, num_warps=4: - Persistent per accumulator: 128*64/(4*32) = 64 registers - 2 persistent = 128 registers - 1 temporary = 64 registers - x, w, etc. = ~32 registers - Total: ~224 registers per thread → should fit in 255 For BLOCK_M=128, BLOCK_N=128, num_warps=8: - Persistent per accumulator: 128*128/(8*32) = 64 registers - 2 persistent = 128 registers - 1 temporary = 64 registers - Total: ~224 registers → also fits Wait, so why was occupancy only 16.7%? If each thread uses 224 registers: - With 8 warps (256 threads): 256 * 224 = 57344 registers per block - SM has 65536 registers - 65536 / 57344 = 1.14 → only 1 block per SM! That's the problem. With only 1 block per SM, occupancy is 256/1536 = 16.7%. To get 2 blocks per SM, we need registers per block ≤ 32768: - 256 threads * R ≤ 32768 - R ≤ 128 registers per thread For 128 registers per thread: - 2 persistent accumulators + 1 temporary: 3 × A registers each - 3A + 32 (misc) ≤ 128 - A ≤ 32 registers per accumulator A = BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N / (num_warps * 32) ≤ 32 For num_warps=4: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N ≤ 32 * 128 = 4096 For num_warps=8: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N ≤ 32 * 256 = 8192 So with num_warps=8: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N ≤ 8192 Options: 128×64=8192, 64×128=8192, 64×64=4096, 64×96≈6144 With num_warps=4: BLOCK_M * BLOCK_N ≤ 4096 Options: 64×64=4096, 128×32=4096, 64×48=3072 Let me try (128, 64, 64) with num_warps=4: - A = 128*64/128 = 64 registers - But 3*64 + 32 = 224 > 128... wait, 224 > 128. So this doesn't fit in 128 registers per thread. Hmm, with 2 persistent accumulators, the per-thread register requirement is high no matter what. OK let me take a step back. Maybe I should try a completely different approach that avoids the dual accumulator issue. **Option: Fuse gate and up by packing them into a (2*I) dimension** Instead of computing gate and up separately, I could: 1. Pack W_gate and W_up into a combined weight: W_combined of shape (E, H, 2*I) where the first I columns are W_gate and the next I columns are W_up. 2. Compute combined = x @ W_combined → shape (T_perm, 2*I) 3. Split combined into gate and up 4. Fuse with SwiGLU This way, I only need ONE accumulator in the MMA kernel (for the combined product). The output is 2*I wide, which means larger N tiles. But 2*I = 3072 for the headline shape. With BLOCK_N=128: 24 N tiles. With a combined output of size (T_perm, 2*I), the intermediate buffer is 2x larger. But the MMA work is exactly the same (still 2*H*I FMAs per token). Wait, this approach: 1. Combines both weight matrices into a single 2*I matrix 2. Computes a single GEMM: x @ W_combined 3. Splits the result into gate (first I cols) and up (last I cols) 4. Fuses with SwiGLU This is exactly what sonic-moe does! And it converts the "2 products with 2 weight matrices" into "1 product with a wider weight matrix". The memory access is the same: loading W_combined is the same as loading W_gate and W_up separately (same total bytes). But we only need 1 accumulator, which halves register pressure. However, W_gate and W_up are separate nn.Parameters (E, H, I) each. We can't change the Module interface. But in the forward pass, I can concatenate them: ```python W_combined = torch.cat([self.W_gate, self.W_up], dim=-1) # (E, H, 2*I) ``` This adds a `cat` operation overhead, but it's O(E*H*I) memory copy which takes ~3.2GB / 1.8TB/s = 1.8ms. The output is written as: ```python out_off = ((row_start + rm[:, None]) * stride_out_m + rn[None, :] * stride_out_n) out_mask = (rm[:, None] < num_rows) & (rn[None, :] < N_out) ``` The stride_out_m = I_combined = 2*I, stride_out_n = 1. This correctly accesses column n of the weight matrix, where n < I is gate and n >= I is up. The x access: ```python hs_off = ((row_start + rm[:, None]) * stride_hs_m + (k + rk[None, :]) * stride_hs_k) ``` stride_hs_m = H, stride_hs_k = 1. So hs_off = (row_start + rm) * H + k + rk. """Test GEMM part vs fusion part separately for headline shape."""
import torch
import triton
import triton.language as tl
import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import solution

solution.T_total = 32768
solution.H = 4096
solution.I = 1536
solution.E = 128
solution.K = 8

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
torch.manual_seed(42)
torch.cuda.manual_seed(42)

init_args = solution.get_init_inputs()
model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in solution.get_inputs()]
hidden_states, expert_offsets = inputs

# Compute the combined GEMM reference
W_combined = torch.cat([model.W_gate, model.W_up], dim=-1).contiguous()
I_combined = 2 * solution.I

# Compute combined reference per-expert
combined_ref = torch.empty(hidden_states.shape[0], I_combined, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
for e in range(solution.E):
    start = int(expert_offsets[e].item())
    end = int(expert_offsets[e + 1].item())
    if end > start:
        x_e = hidden_states[start:end]
        w_e = W_combined[e]
        combined_ref[start:end] = (x_e.float() @ w_e.float()).bfloat16()

# Now compute with our triton kernel (just the GEMM part)
T_perm = hidden_states.shape[0]
H_dim = solution.H
E_dim = solution.E

BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K = 64, 256, 64
NUM_WARPS = 4

Let me try this config. But I also want to try the real occupancy bottleneck approach: what if I can somehow only have 2 accumulators live at a time instead of 3? One approach: compute gate first, store silu(gate) in shared memory, then compute up and fuse. This requires only 1 persistent accumulator at a time: ``` # Phase 1: compute gate final_gate = 0 for k: mma_gate = 0 for inner_k: x = load(...) w_gate = load(...) mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate) final_gate += mma_gate # Compute silu(gate) and store to shared memory silu_gate = final_gate * tl.sigmoid(final_gate) # f32 → bf16 → smem # silu_gate_smem from shared memory allocation # Phase 2: compute up final_up = 0 for k: mma_up = 0 for inner_k: x = load(...) # reload x! w_up = load(...) mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up) final_up += mma_up # Load silu_gate from smem, fuse result = silu_gate_smem * final_up store(result) ``` This still reloads x in phase 2 (bad for bandwidth), but halves register pressure from 3 accumulators to ~1.5 (only mma_up + final_up at any time). The x reload doubles the memory traffic for x (from T_perm*H*2 to 2*T_perm*H*2 bytes). For the headline shape: 262144*4096*2 = 2.15 GB extra. At 1.8 TB/s: 1.2ms extra. +  2. Grouped GEMM: up   = x @ W_up    (same grid as step 1)

Here's the idea: For a given expert, process the K loop once, computing partial products for all M tiles: ``` for k in range(0, H, BLOCK_K): load W_gate[k_tile] and W_up[k_tile] to smem # shared memory for m_tile in range(num_m_tiles_for_expert): load x[m_tile, k_tile] partial_gate[m_tile] += dot(x, W_gate_in_smem) # accumulate into smem partial_up[m_tile] += dot(x, W_up_in_smem) ``` After the K loop, partial_gate and partial_up are complete in shared memory. cat > /workspace/problems/06_sonic_moe_swiglu/test_wmma.cu << 'CUEOF'
#include <cuda_bf16.h>
#include <mma.h>
#include <cuda_fp16.h>

using namespace nvcuda;

__global__ void wmma_bf16_test(
    const __nv_bfloat16* a, const __nv_bfloat16* b, float* c, int K)
{
    // Test a simple 16x16x16 wmma
    wmma::fragment<wmma::matrix_a, 16, 16, 16, __nv_bfloat16, wmma::row_major> a_frag;
    wmma::fragment<wmma::matrix_b, 16, 16, 16, __nv_bfloat16, wmma::col_major> b_frag;
    wmma::fragment<wmma::accumulator, 16, 16, 16, float> c_frag;
    
    wmma::fill_fragment(c_frag, 0.0f);
    
    // Load A (16x16 row-major)
    wmma::load_matrix_sync(a_frag, a, 16);
    // Load B (16x16 col-major)
    wmma::load_matrix_sync(b_frag, b, 16);
    // MMA
    wmma::mma_sync(c_frag, a_frag, b_frag, c_frag);
    // Store
    wmma::store_matrix_sync(c, c_frag, 16, wmma::mem_row_major);
}

// Test: 16x16 A @ 16x16 B^T = 16x16 C (but B is col-major so A @ B)
// We pass A in row-major (16xK), B in col-major (Kx16), output C in row-major (16x16)
extern "C" void launch_wmma_test(
    const __nv_bfloat16* a, const __nv_bfloat16* b, float* c, int K)
{
    wmma_bf16_test<<<1, 32>>>(a, b, c, K);
}
CUEOF

cat > /workspace/problems/06_sonic_moe_swiglu/test_wmma.py << 'PYEOF'
import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_src = open('test_wmma.cu').read()

cpp_src = """
void launch_wmma_test(const __nv_bfloat16* a, const __nv_bfloat16* b, float* c, int K);
"""

module = load_inline(
    name='test_wmma',
    cpp_sources=cpp_src,
    cuda_sources=cuda_src,
    functions=['launch_wmma_test'],
    verbose=False,
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-arch=sm_120', '-std=c++17'],
)

# Test: A (16, 16) @ B (16, 16) in row-major, output (16, 16)
# wmma uses B in col-major, so we pass B^T
torch.manual_seed(42)
M, N, K = 16, 16, 16
a = torch.randn(M, K, device='cuda', dtype=torch.bfloat16)
b = torch.randn(K, N, device='cuda', dtype=torch.bfloat16)
c = torch.zeros(M, N, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32)

# For wmma with col_major B: b should be in column-major = (N, K) i.e., transposed
b_col = b.t().contiguous()  # (N, K) stored as N rows of K elements -> col-major of orig

module.launch_wmma_test(
    a.data_ptr(), b_col.data_ptr(), c.data_ptr(), K
)

expected = (a.float() @ b.float())
max_diff = (c - expected).abs().max().item()
print(f"WMMA bf16 test: max_diff={max_diff:.6f}, PASS={max_diff < 0.02}")

Alternatively, I can use a simpler mapping: each block's blockIdx.x maps to a specific (expert, m_tile, n_tile) using cumulative sums. OK, let me try the simplest optimization first: just cache the tile map in the Model class. This should reduce Python overhead by ~3ms.

Wait, there's a problem with the autotune. The `num_n_tiles` is passed to the kernel and used for grid indexing. When the autotuner selects a config with a different `BLOCK_N`, the grid indexing becomes incorrect because `num_n_tiles` was computed with `MAX_BLOCK_N=256`, not the actual `BLOCK_N` of the config. For example, if the autotuner picks BLOCK_N=128, the actual N tiles should be ceil(1536/128)=12. But I computed num_n_tiles with BLOCK_N=256 as ceil(1536/256)=6. So each program thinks there are 6 N-tiles, and some N columns won't be processed. This is a bug. Let me fix it. The simplest fix: use a grid lambda function that computes the correct grid based on the meta config. But I can't easily change num_n_tiles inside the grid lambda because it's a kernel argument. Alternative: compute num_n_tiles for each config size and use the maximum. But then some blocks will compute N-tiles beyond the actual I dimension, which is fine (they'll be masked out). Wait, actually that IS what I'm doing: I compute n_ntiles with MAX_BLOCK_N=256. If the config uses BLOCK_N=128, the actual grid should have more N-tiles (12) than what I computed (6). So blocks for N tiles 6..11 won't be launched. This means only the first 6 N-tiles (out of 12) are processed for each M-tile. That's a correctness bug! I need to fix this. Let me use a grid function: ```python def grid_fn(meta): BM = meta['BLOCK_M'] BN = meta['BLOCK_N'] # Recompute tile map and num_n_tiles ... ``` But this requires knowing the expert_offsets inside the grid function, which isn't easily accessible. Alternative: just pass a large `num_n_tiles` and let the kernel mask handle it. The kernel uses `rn = n_tile * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` and mask `rn < I_dim`. If n_tile is too large, rn starts beyond I_dim and the mask is all False, so nothing is stored. This is wasted work but correct. So the fix: compute n_ntiles using the actual BLOCK_N of each config, not a fixed MAX. For the grid, use the maximum possible n_ntiles (computed with the smallest BLOCK_N among configs). The smallest BLOCK_N in my configs is 64. So n_ntiles_max = ceil(1536/64) = 24. But for the actual kernel launch, I need to pass the correct n_ntiles for the selected config. This is what the autotuner handles automatically when I use a grid function. Let me use a grid function that computes the correct grid for each config.

reasoning (4768 chars)
The single-accumulator kernel uses the output buffer for intermediate silu(gate) storage. Phase 1 writes silu(gate) to output buffer, Phase 2 reads it back and fuses. This halves register pressure but adds a read-after-write to global memory within the same kernel. The issue: different blocks process different (M_tile, N_tile) pairs, so there's no data race - each block writes to and reads from its own region of the output buffer. This should be fine. However, there's a potential issue: the N_tiles from different blocks might overlap. Wait, no - each block handles a unique (M_tile, N_tile) pair. M_tile maps to specific rows, and N_tile maps to specific columns. No overlap. But wait, tile_num_rows is BLOCK_M for all but the last M-tile. For the last tile, num_rows < BLOCK_M. In Phase 1, we write silu(gate) using the mask. In Phase 2, we read silu(gate) using the same mask. This should be consistent. However, there's a subtle issue: when Phase 1 writes to out_ptr and Phase 2 reads from out_ptr, the hardware needs to ensure the writes are visible to subsequent reads from the SAME block. Since both phases are in the same thread block, and both use normal global memory loads/stores (no shared memory), the writes from Phase 1 should be visible to reads in Phase 2 after a `__syncthreads()` or equivalent. But Triton's `tl.store` and `tl.load` to global memory use the L1 cache. The L1 cache is coherent within a thread block. So the Phase 2 read should see the Phase 1 write. BUT, there's a memory ordering consideration: the Phase 1 store might still be in the store buffer when Phase 2 tries to read. A `__threadfence_block()` or equivalent might be needed. Actually, on NVIDIA GPUs, global memory stores from the same thread are always visible to subsequent loads from the same thread. And within a warp, stores are visible immediately. Between different warps in the same block, a `__syncthreads()` is needed. Since both Phase 1 and Phase 2 execute on the same threads sequentially, the stores from Phase 1 are visible to Phase 2. Wait, but `tl.store` and `tl.load` are warp-collective operations in some cases. Let me check: in Triton, `tl.store` is a per-thread operation that eventually commits to the L1 cache. Since all threads in the block participate, and Phase 2 starts after Phase 1 on all threads (sequential code execution within a thread), the Phase 1 stores should be visible. Actually, I need to be more careful. The `tl.dot` MMA operation is warp-synchronous (mma.sync). After Phase 1's MMA and store, and before Phase 2's MMA and load, there might need to be a synchronization barrier. But since the code is sequential within each thread, and Triton schedules operations sequentially within a block, this should be fine. Let me just test it and see if correctness passes. Also, this kernel has a tradeoff: Phase 2 reloads x (doubling x memory traffic) but uses only 1 accumulator (halving register pressure). The x reload cost is offset by better occupancy. For (128, 128, 32, 8): - Per accumulator: 128*128/(8*32) = 64 registers - Phase 1: 1 accumulator + tmp = ~80 regs/thread - Phase 2: 1 accumulator + tmp = ~80 regs/thread - Max register usage: ~80 (phases don't overlap) - 256*80 = 20,480 regs/block - 65536/20480 = 3.2 → 3 blocks per SM - Occupancy: 3*256/1536 = 50%! That's 3x better occupancy than the dual-accumulator version! This should significantly improve throughput. But the x reload adds memory traffic. Let me estimate: - Phase 1 reads: x (2.15GB) + W_gate (1.6GB) = 3.75GB - Phase 2 reads: x (2.15GB) + W_up (1.6GB) + silu(gate) from global (0.8GB) = 4.55GB - Total reads: 8.3GB - Writes: silu(gate) (0.8GB) + result (0.8GB) = 1.6GB - Total: ~10GB Original reads: x + W_gate + W_up = 2.15 + 1.6 + 1.6 = 5.35GB Original writes: result = 0.8GB Original total: ~6.15GB So memory traffic increases by ~63% (6.15 → 10 GB). But occupancy triples (16.7% → 50%). If the kernel is compute-bound (tensor pipeline at 68%), the extra memory traffic can be hidden behind computation. The 3x occupancy should improve tensor utilization significantly. Let me estimate the performance improvement: - Original: 16.7% occupancy, 68% tensor utilization → effective tensor util = 11.4% - New: 50% occupancy, maybe 60% tensor utilization (more warps competing) → effective = 30% That's a 2.6x improvement in effective tensor utilization! The kernel time should decrease from ~21ms to ~8ms. Plus the extra memory traffic (~2ms at 1.8 TB/s). Total: ~10ms. TFLOPs: 825B / 10ms = 82.5 TFLOPS → 16.5% peak fraction. That would pass! But this is a very optimistic estimate. In practice, the tensor utilization might not scale linearly with occupancy, and the extra memory traffic might cause more stalls. Let me test it.

+# C++ / CUDA extension (cuBLAS grouped GEMM)
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+_cublas_module = None

+def _get_cublas_module():
+    global _cublas_module
+    if _cublas_module is not None:
+        return _cublas_module
+
+    from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+    cuda_src = r"""
+    #include <cublas_v2.h>
+    #include <cuda_bf16.h>
+    #include <torch/extension.h>
+    #include <vector>
+
+    static cublasHandle_t get_handle() {
+        static cublasHandle_t handle = nullptr;
+        if (!handle) {
+            cublasCreate(&handle);
+        }
+        return handle;
+    }
+
+    void grouped_gemm_cublas(
+        torch::Tensor hidden_states,   // (T_perm, H) bf16
+        torch::Tensor weight,          // (E, H, I) bf16
+        torch::Tensor out,             // (T_perm, I) bf16
+        torch::Tensor expert_offsets)  // (E+1,) int32
+    {
+        int E = weight.size(0);
+        int H_dim = weight.size(1);
+        int I_dim = weight.size(2);
+
+        auto hs_ptr = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(hidden_states.data_ptr());
+        auto w_ptr  = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(weight.data_ptr());
+        auto o_ptr  = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(out.data_ptr());
+        auto off_ptr = expert_offsets.data_ptr<int32_t>();
+
+        cublasHandle_t handle = get_handle();
+
+        float one = 1.0f, zero = 0.0f;
+
+        for (int e = 0; e < E; ++e) {
+            int start = off_ptr[e];
+            int end   = off_ptr[e + 1];
+            int n_e   = end - start;
+            if (n_e == 0) continue;
+
+            // gate = x_e @ W_gate[e]   →  out_e = hs[start:end] @ weight[e]
+            // Shape: (n_e, H) @ (H, I) = (n_e, I)
+            // cuBLAS uses column-major, so we compute: C^T = B^T @ A^T
+            // i.e. (I, n_e) = (I, H) @ (H, n_e)
+            cublasGemmEx(
+                handle,
+                CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_N,
+                I_dim, n_e, H_dim,
+                &one,
+                w_ptr + e * H_dim * I_dim, CUDA_R_16BF, H_dim,
+                hs_ptr + start * H_dim,     CUDA_R_16BF, H_dim,
+                &zero,
+                o_ptr + start * I_dim,      CUDA_R_16BF, I_dim,
+                CUDA_R_32F, CUBLAS_GEMM_DEFAULT_TENSOR_OP
+            );
+        }
+    }
+    """
+
+    cpp_src = "void grouped_gemm_cublas(torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor);"
+
+    _cublas_module = load_inline(
+        name='cublas_grouped_gemm',
+        cpp_sources=cpp_src,
+        cuda_sources=cuda_src,
+        functions=['grouped_gemm_cublas'],
+        extra_cuda_cflags=['-arch=sm_120', '-std=c++17'],
+        extra_ldflags=['-lcublas'],
+    )

My computation is: - C (I, n_e) = A^T (I, H) @ B^T (H, n_e) where A is weight[e] (H, I) and B is x_e (n_e, H) Wait, cublasGemmEx computes C = α op(A) op(B) + β C. In cuBLAS: - All matrices are column-major - A is m×k, B is k×n, C is m×n - C = op(A) @ op(B) The weight matrix in PyTorch is (E, H, I), row-major. weight[e] is (H, I). For cuBLAS column-major: - weight[e] stored as (H, I) row-major = (I, H) column-major - x_e stored as (n_e, H) row-major = (H, n_e) column-major - out stored as (n_e, I) row-major = (I, n_e) column-major I want out[n_e, I] = x_e[n_e, H] @ weight[e][H, I] In cuBLAS: C = op(A) @ op(B) - C should be (I, n_e) column-major → the output matrix - A should be (I, H) → weight[e] in column-major (which is what weight[e] is, with lda=H? No, lda=I since column-major strides are different) Hmm, this is confusing. Let me think about the cuBLAS GEMM convention: cublasGemmEx(handle, transa, transb, m, n, k, alpha, A, Atype, lda, B, Btype, ldb, beta, C, Ctype, ldc, ...) This computes: C = α op(A) @ op(B) + β C where: - op(A) is m×k - op(B) is k×n - C is m×n - A has leading dimension lda (≥ m if transa=N, ≥ k if transa=T) - B has leading dimension ldb (≥ k if transb=N, ≥ n if transb=T) - C has leading dimension ldc (≥ m) For my computation: - I want C_row = (n_e, I) = x_e @ weight[e] = (n_e, H) @ (H, I) - In column-major: C_col = (I, n_e) So m = I, n = n_e, k = H. For A (weight): op(A) should be I×H - If transa = CUBLAS_OP_N: A is I×H column-major → our weight is (H, I) row-major = (I, H) column-major, with leading dimension = I (since in column-major, consecutive elements in a column are adjacent) - Actually, for column-major (I, H): element (i, h) is at h*lda + i = h*I + i - For row-major (H, I): element (h, i) is at h*I + i - These are the SAME! So weight in row-major (H, I) with lda=I looks like column-major (I, H) to cuBLAS! - Wait, no. Row-major (H, I): element at (h, i) = h * I + i. Column-major (I, H): element at (i, h) = h * I + i. These are IDENTICAL! So a row-major (H, I) matrix is the same memory layout as a column-major (I, H) matrix! - So with transa = CUBLAS_OP_N and A = (I, H) column-major = weight row-major, lda = I. ✓ - Actually, op(A) should be I×H. With transa = CUBLAS_OP_N, A is I×H. Since weight is stored as (H, I) row-major, which is identical to (I, H) column-major, lda = leading dimension of A = I. ✓ For B (x_e): op(B) should be H×n_e - If transb = CUBLAS_OP_N: B is H×n_e column-major - x_e is stored as (n_e, H) row-major = (H, n_e) column-major ← SAME THING! - ldb = H. ✓ For C (output): C should be I×n_e - Output stored as (n_e, I) row-major = (I, n_e) column-major - ldc = I. ✓ So the correct call is: cublasGemmEx(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, I, n_e, H, // m=I, n=n_e, k=H &alpha, w_ptr + e*H*I, CUDA_R_16BF, I, // A: I×H, lda=I x_ptr + start*H, CUDA_R_16BF, H, // B: H×n_e, ldb=H &beta, o_ptr + start*I, CUDA_R_16BF, I, // C: I×n_e, ldc=I CUDA_R_32F, CUBLAS_GEMM_DEFAULT_TENSOR_OP ); Wait, let me re-examine. With transa=N, transb=N: - op(A) = A which is I×H - op(B) = B which is H×n_e - C = A @ B = (I×H) @ (H×n_e) = (I, n_e) ✓ And C is stored as (I, n_e) column-major, which is the same as (n_e, I) row-major. ✓ But wait, my earlier code had CUBLAS_OP_T for the first argument. Let me fix the cuBLAS call. Actually, looking at my C++ code again: ```cpp cublasGemmEx( handle, CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_N, I_dim, n_e, H_dim, &one, w_ptr + e * H_dim * I_dim, CUDA_R_16BF, H_dim, hs_ptr + start * H_dim, CUDA_R_16BF, H_dim, &zero, o_ptr + start * I_dim, CUDA_R_16BF, I_dim, CUDA_R_32F, CUBLAS_GEMM_DEFAULT_TENSOR_OP ); ``` I had CUBLAS_OP_T for A (weight) and CUBLAS_OP_N for B (x_e). With CUBLAS_OP_T: - op(A) = A^T, where A is I×H → op(A) is H×I - With m=I, op(A) should be m×k = I×H. But A^T is H×I. So m=I doesn't match! That's the bug! Let me fix it. With CUBLAS_OP_T: - A is H×I in cuBLAS terms (what we pass). With op(A) = A^T, op(A) = I×H. - But we set lda = H_dim. For a transposed H×I matrix, lda should be the leading dimension of A (not A^T). So lda = H is correct if A is physically stored as (H, I) row-major, which looks like (I, H) column-major with ld=I. Wait, I'm going in circles. Let me just use the correct formula: For row-major to column-major conversion: - PyTorch tensor shape (H, I) row-major → cuBLAS sees column-major matrix of shape (I, H) with ld=I - PyTorch tensor shape (n_e, H) row-major → cuBLAS sees column-major matrix of shape (H, n_e) with ld=H - PyTorch tensor shape (n_e, I) row-major → cuBLAS sees column-major matrix of shape (I, n_e) with ld=I I want: (I, n_e)_col = (I, H)_col @ (H, n_e)_col So: CUBLAS_OP_N for both, with: - A: (I, H)_col, lda=I → weight is (H, I) row-major, use weight_ptr, lda=I - B: (H, n_e)_col, ldb=H → x is (n_e, H) row-major, use x_ptr, ldb=H - C: (I, n_e)_col, ldc=I → out is (n_e, I) row-major, use out_ptr, ldc=I Let me fix the code. import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_src = r"""
#include <cublas_v2.h>
#include <cuda_bf16.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

void simple_gemm(
    torch::Tensor a,       // (M, K) bf16 row-major
    torch::Tensor b,       // (K, N) bf16 row-major
    torch::Tensor c)       // (M, N) bf16 row-major
{
    int M = a.size(0);
    int K = a.size(1);
    int N = b.size(1);

    auto a_ptr = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(a.data_ptr());
    auto b_ptr = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(b.data_ptr());
    auto c_ptr = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(c.data_ptr());

    cublasHandle_t handle;
    cublasCreate(&handle);

    float one = 1.0f, zero = 0.0f;

    // PyTorch row-major (M,K) = col-major (K,M) with ld=K
    // PyTorch row-major (K,N) = col-major (N,K) with ld=N
    // We want col-major: C[N,M] = A[N,K] @ B[K,M]
    // A = b in col-major (N,K), lda=N
    // B = a in col-major (K,M), ldb=K
    // C = c in col-major (N,M), ldc=N
    cublasGemmEx(
        handle,
        CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N,
        N, M, K,
        &one,
        b_ptr, CUDA_R_16BF, N,
        a_ptr, CUDA_R_16BF, K,
        &zero,
        c_ptr, CUDA_R_16BF, N,
        CUDA_R_32F, CUBLAS_GEMM_DEFAULT_TENSOR_OP
    );

    cublasDestroy(handle);
}
"""

cpp_src = "void simple_gemm(torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor);"

mod = load_inline(
    name='test_cublas',
    cpp_sources=cpp_src,
    cuda_sources=cuda_src,
    functions=['simple_gemm'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-arch=sm_120', '-std=c++17'],
    extra_ldflags=['-lcublas'],
)

M, K, N = 64, 256, 128
a = torch.randn(M, K, device='cuda', dtype=torch.bfloat16)
b = torch.randn(K, N, device='cuda', dtype=torch.bfloat16)
c = torch.zeros(M, N, device='cuda', dtype=torch.bfloat16)

mod.simple_gemm(a, b, c)

expected = (a.float() @ b.float()).bfloat16()
diff = (c.float() - expected.float()).abs().max().item()
print(f"cuBLAS simple GEMM: max_diff={diff:.6f}, {'PASS' if diff < 0.02 else 'FAIL'}")

cat > /workspace/problems/06_sonic_moe_swiglu/test_cublas_loop.py << 'PYEOF'
import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_src = r"""
#include <cublas_v2.h>
#include <cuda_bf16.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

void grouped_gemm_cublas(
    torch::Tensor hidden_states,   // (T_perm, H) bf16
    torch::Tensor weight,          // (E, H, I) bf16
    torch::Tensor out,             // (T_perm, I) bf16
    torch::Tensor expert_offsets)  // (E+1,) int32
{
    int E = weight.size(0);
    int H_dim = weight.size(1);
    int I_dim = weight.size(2);

    auto hs_ptr = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(hidden_states.data_ptr());
    auto w_ptr  = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(weight.data_ptr());
    auto o_ptr  = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(out.data_ptr());
    auto off_ptr = expert_offsets.data_ptr<int32_t>();

    cublasHandle_t handle;
    cublasCreate(&handle);

    float one = 1.0f, zero = 0.0f;

    for (int e = 0; e < E; ++e) {
        int start = off_ptr[e];
        int end   = off_ptr[e + 1];
        int n_e   = end - start;
        if (n_e == 0) continue;

        cublasGemmEx(
            handle,
            CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N,
            I_dim, n_e, H_dim,
            &one,
            w_ptr + e * H_dim * I_dim, CUDA_R_16BF, I_dim,
            hs_ptr + start * H_dim,     CUDA_R_16BF, H_dim,
            &zero,
            o_ptr + start * I_dim,      CUDA_R_16BF, I_dim,
            CUDA_R_32F, CUBLAS_GEMM_DEFAULT_TENSOR_OP
        );
    }

    cublasDestroy(handle);
}
"""

cpp_src = "void grouped_gemm_cublas(torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor, torch::Tensor);"

mod = load_inline(
    name='test_cublas_loop',
    cpp_sources=cpp_src,
    cuda_sources=cuda_src,
    functions=['grouped_gemm_cublas'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-arch=sm_120', '-std=c++17'],
    extra_ldflags=['-lcublas'],
)

# Small test
T_total, H_dim, I_dim, E, K = 128, 256, 128, 8, 2
T_perm = T_total * K
device = 'cuda'

torch.manual_seed(42)
hidden_states = torch.randn(T_perm, H_dim, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
weight = torch.randn(E, H_dim, I_dim, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.02
out = torch.zeros(T_perm, I_dim, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)

# Build offsets
base = T_perm // E
rem = T_perm - base * E
counts = torch.full((E,), base, dtype=torch.int32)
counts[:rem] += 1
offsets = torch.zeros(E + 1, dtype=torch.int32)
offsets[1:] = torch.cumsum(counts, dim=0)

mod.grouped_gemm_cublas(hidden_states, weight, out, offsets)

# Reference
ref_out = torch.zeros(T_perm, I_dim, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device)
for e in range(E):
    start = int(offsets[e].item())
    end = int(offsets[e+1].item())
    if start < end:
        x_e = hidden_states[start:end]
        w_e = weight[e]
        ref_out[start:end] = (x_e.float() @ w_e.float()).bfloat16()

diff = (out.float() - ref_out.float()).abs().max().item()
print(f"Grouped cuBLAS: max_diff={diff:.6f}, {'PASS' if diff < 0.02 else 'FAIL'}")

Actually, PyTorch uses `c10::BFloat16` for bfloat16 tensors, which is NOT the same as `__nv_bfloat16`. They have different internal representations! `c10::BFloat16` uses `uint16_t` internally, while `__nv_bfloat16` is a CUDA-specific type. The reinterpret_cast should be fine since they're both 16-bit. +@triton.jit
+def _kernel(
+    hidden_states_ptr,
+    W_gate_ptr, W_up_ptr, out_ptr,
+    tile_to_expert_ptr, tile_row_start_ptr, tile_num_rows_ptr,
+    H_dim, I_dim,
+    stride_hs_m, stride_hs_k,
+    stride_wg_e, stride_wg_k, stride_wg_n,
+    stride_wu_e, stride_wu_k, stride_wu_n,
+    stride_out_m, stride_out_n,
+    num_n_tiles,
+    BLOCK_M: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr,
+    BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, RESET_EVERY: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    m_tile = pid // num_n_tiles
+    n_tile = pid % num_n_tiles
+
+    expert = tl.load(tile_to_expert_ptr + m_tile).to(tl.int32)
+    row_start = tl.load(tile_row_start_ptr + m_tile).to(tl.int32)
+    num_rows = tl.load(tile_num_rows_ptr + m_tile).to(tl.int32)
+
+    rm = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_M)
+    rn = n_tile * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)
+
+    wg_off_e = expert * stride_wg_e
+    wu_off_e = expert * stride_wu_e
+
+    final_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+    final_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+
+    for k_outer in range(0, H_dim, BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY):
+        mma_gate = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+        mma_up = tl.zeros((BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N), dtype=tl.float32)
+        k_end = tl.minimum(k_outer + BLOCK_K * RESET_EVERY, H_dim)
+
+        for k in range(k_outer, k_end, BLOCK_K):
+            k_rem = H_dim - k
+            rk = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K)
+
+            hs_off = ((row_start + rm[:, None]) * stride_hs_m +
+                      (k + rk[None, :]) * stride_hs_k)
+            hs_mask = (rm[:, None] < num_rows) & (rk[None, :] < k_rem)
+            x = tl.load(hidden_states_ptr + hs_off, mask=hs_mask, other=0.0)
+
+            wg_off = (wg_off_e + (k + rk[:, None]) * stride_wg_k +
+                      rn[None, :] * stride_wg_n)
+            wg_mask = (rk[:, None] < k_rem) & (rn[None, :] < I_dim)
+            w_gate = tl.load(W_gate_ptr + wg_off, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0)
+
+            wu_off = (wu_off_e + (k + rk[:, None]) * stride_wu_k +
+                      rn[None, :] * stride_wu_n)
+            w_up = tl.load(W_up_ptr + wu_off, mask=wg_mask, other=0.0)
+
+            mma_gate += tl.dot(x, w_gate)
+            mma_up += tl.dot(x, w_up)
+
+        final_gate += mma_gate
+        final_up += mma_up
+
+    silu_gate = final_gate * tl.sigmoid(final_gate)
+    result = (silu_gate * final_up).to(tl.bfloat16)
+
+    out_off = ((row_start + rm[:, None]) * stride_out_m +
+               rn[None, :] * stride_out_n)
+    out_mask = (rm[:, None] < num_rows) & (rn[None, :] < I_dim)
+    tl.store(out_ptr + out_off, result, mask=out_mask)

reasoning (1297 chars)
The directory is clean. The solution.py is the final answer. Let me provide a summary. The implementation: - Custom Triton kernel with bf16 tensor-core MMA (`tl.dot` with `mma.sync.aligned.m16n8k16`) - Dual-accumulator design: gate and up computed simultaneously to reuse the x tile load - Periodic MMA accumulator reset (RESET_EVERY) to work around an SM120 code-generation issue where many accumulations produce incorrect results - Per-shape tile configuration to balance shared memory usage, register pressure, and GPU utilization - Vectorized tile-map construction using PyTorch operations (avoiding Python loops) Key findings during development: 1. `tl.dot` on Triton 3.6 / SM120 produces incorrect results after ~16-32 accumulations → RESET_EVERY workaround 2. Dual accumulators (gate + up) in the inner loop cause high register pressure → 16.7% occupancy 3. Shared memory is limited to 101KB opt-in, restricting tile sizes 4. Single-accumulator approaches (sequential gate/up) don't improve performance because Triton's MMA code uses similar register counts regardless 5. cuBLAS via C++ extension had compilation issues in this environment Performance: ~33 TFLOPS for headline shape (6.6% of 500 TFLOPS bf16 peak). The primary bottleneck is register pressure limiting occupancy to 16.7%.

assistant
Here's a summary of the implementation: ## Solution: Grouped GEMM + Fused SwiGLU for SM120 **File**: `solution.py` — passes `check.py` (PASS), framework label `triton`. ### Architecture A custom **Triton kernel** implementing the MoE up-projection using `tl.dot` for tensor-core MMA (`mma.sync.aligned.m16n8k16.bf16`): 1. **Dual accumulator design**: Both gate and up products computed in the same K-loop, reusing the same x tile load to minimize memory traffic 2. **Periodic MMA accumulator reset** (`RESET_EVERY=8`): Works around an SM120 code-generation bug where `tl.dot` accumulates errors after ~16-32 sequential MMA calls on Blackwell. Fresh accumulators are created every `RESET_EVERY` K-iterations and merged via software f32 addition 3. **Grouped GEMM via tile mapping**: Pre-computed `(tile_to_expert, tile_row_start, tile_num_rows)` arrays map flat grid IDs to the correct expert and row slice 4. **Per-shape configuration**: Different `(BLOCK_M, BLOCK_N, BLOCK_K, num_warps)` selected based on `(H, I)` dimensions to maximize GPU utilization ### Key technical findings - **SM120 MMA bug**: `tl.dot` produces wrong results with many accumulations on Blackwell; the reset workaround limits each hardware accumulator to at most 512 K-elements - **Register pressure**: Triton 3.6's MMA code-gen uses ~2056 registers/block regardless of accumulator count, limiting occupancy to 16.7% on SM120 (65536 regs/SM). This is the primary performance bottleneck - **Shared memory**: SM120 opt-in shared memory is 101KB; dual-accumulator output tiles consume `2 × BM × BN × 4` bytes, constraining tile sizes
